    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome sequence of &#8216;Kurodagosun&#8217;, a major carrot variety in Japan and China, reveals insights into biological research and carrot breeding"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Molecular Genetics and Genomics 2018 293:4"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Springer"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-03-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Carrot (Daucus carota L.) is one of the most economically important root vegetables in the world, providing numerous nutrients for human health. China is the largest country of carrot production in the world, and &#8216;Kurodagosun&#8217; has been a major carrot variety in China. Carrot material used in this study was the inbred line &#8216;DC-27&#8217;, which was derived by forced selfing from &#8216;Kurodagosun&#8217;. To understand the genetic system and plant-specific genes of &#8216;Kurodagosun&#8217;, we report the draft genome sequence of carrot &#8216;DC-27&#8217; assembled using a combination of Roche454 and HiSeq 2000 sequencing technologies to achieve 32-fold genome coverage. A total of 31,891 predicted genes were identified. These assembled sequences provide candidate genes involved in biological processes including stress response and carotenoid biosynthesis. Genomic sequences corresponding to 371.6&#160;Mb was less than 473&#160;Mb, which is the estimated genome size. The availability of a draft sequence of the &#8216;DC-27&#8217; genome advances knowledge on the biological research and breeding of carrot, as well as other Apiaceae plants. The &#8216;DC-27&#8217; genome sequence data also provide a new resource to explore the evolution of other higher plants."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Molecular Genetics and Genomics"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="The genome sequence of ‘Kurodagosun’, a major carrot variety in Japan and China, reveals insights into biological research and carrot breeding"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Carrot (Daucus carota L.) is one of the most economically important root vegetables in the world, providing numerous nutrients for human health. China is the largest country of carrot production in the world, and ‘Kurodagosun’ has been a major carrot variety in China. Carrot material used in this study was the inbred line ‘DC-27’, which was derived by forced selfing from ‘Kurodagosun’. To understand the genetic system and plant-specific genes of ‘Kurodagosun’, we report the draft genome sequence of carrot ‘DC-27’ assembled using a combination of Roche454 and HiSeq 2000 sequencing technologies to achieve 32-fold genome coverage. A total of 31,891 predicted genes were identified. These assembled sequences provide candidate genes involved in biological processes including stress response and carotenoid biosynthesis. Genomic sequences corresponding to 371.6&amp;nbsp;Mb was less than 473&amp;nbsp;Mb, which is the estimated genome size. The availability of a draft sequence of the ‘DC-27’ genome advances knowledge on the biological research and breeding of carrot, as well as other Apiaceae plants. The ‘DC-27’ genome sequence data also provide a new resource to explore the evolution of other higher plants."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/438.jpg"/>

        function OptanonWrapper() {
            var elementInside = function(candidate, element) {
                if (candidate === element) {
                    return true;
                } else if (candidate.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'body') {
                    return false;
                } else {
                    return elementInside(candidate.parentNode, element);

            };

                }
            } else {

            }

                <div data-article-body="true" data-track-component="article body" class="c-article-body">
                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Carrot (<i>Daucus carota</i> L.) is one of the most economically important root vegetables in the world, providing numerous nutrients for human health. China is the largest country of carrot production in the world, and ‘Kurodagosun’ has been a major carrot variety in China. Carrot material used in this study was the inbred line ‘DC-27’, which was derived by forced selfing from ‘Kurodagosun’. To understand the genetic system and plant-specific genes of ‘Kurodagosun’, we report the draft genome sequence of carrot ‘DC-27’ assembled using a combination of Roche454 and HiSeq 2000 sequencing technologies to achieve 32-fold genome coverage. A total of 31,891 predicted genes were identified. These assembled sequences provide candidate genes involved in biological processes including stress response and carotenoid biosynthesis. Genomic sequences corresponding to 371.6 Mb was less than 473 Mb, which is the estimated genome size. The availability of a draft sequence of the ‘DC-27’ genome advances knowledge on the biological research and breeding of carrot, as well as other Apiaceae plants. The ‘DC-27’ genome sequence data also provide a new resource to explore the evolution of other higher plants.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    

                    

                    
                        
                            <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Carrot (<i>Daucus carota</i> L.) is one of the most economically important root vegetables in the world. The most commonly consumed part of carrot is the taproot. The popularity of carrot is attributed to its pleasing appearance and flavor, long shelf life and affordable price. Carrot is one of the most widely cultivated vegetables in the world, ranking among the top 10 vegetable crops. At present, around 100 countries grow carrots, with China being the world’s largest carrot-producing country, accounting for ~ 40% of worldwide carrot production in 2014. According to the Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations, over 20 million tons of carrots are produced every year on about one million hectares (<a href="http://faostat.fao.org">http://faostat.fao.org</a>).</p><p>Consumption of carrot has steadily increased in recent years because of its numerous nutrients, such as carotenoids, anthocyanins, sugar, protein, and fiber (Hedrén et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2002" title="Hedrén E, Mulokozi G, Svanberg U (2002) In vitro accessibility of carotenes from green leafy vegetables cooked with sunflower oil or red palm oil. Int J Food Sci Nutr 53:445–453" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d57061e427">2002</a>). Carrots are an important source of carotenoids and provitamin A in the human diet (Heinonen <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1990" title="Heinonen MI (1990) Carotenoids and provitamin A activity of carrot (Daucus carota L.) cultivars. J Agr Food Chem 38:609–612" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d57061e430">1990</a>). Anthocyanins are also found in carrot, and their presence in various concentrations along with carotenoids accounts for the different colors of carrot (Charron et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2009" title="Charron CS, Kurilich AC, Clevidence BA, Simon PW, Harrison DJ, Britz SJ, Baer DJ, Novotny JA (2009) Bioavailability of anthocyanins from purple carrot juice: effects of acylation and plant matrix. J Agr Food Chem 57:1226–1230" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d57061e433">2009</a>).</p><p>The first complete genome sequence of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, a model dicot flowering plant, was reported in 2000 (Initiative <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2000" title="Initiative TAG (2000) Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408:796–815" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d57061e442">2000</a>). Two years later, the genome of rice, another important model monocot plant, was also published (Goff et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2002" title="Goff SA, Ricke D, Lan TH, Presting G, Wang R, Dunn M, Glazebrook J, Sessions A, Oeller P, Varma H, Hadley D, Hutchison D, Martin C, Katagiri F, Lange BM, Moughamer T, Xia Y, Budworth P, Zhong J, Miguel T, Paszkowski U, Zhang S, Colbert M, Sun WL, Chen L, Cooper B, Park S, Wood TC, Mao L, Quail P, Wing R, Dean R, Yu Y, Zharkikh A, Shen R, Sahasrabudhe S, Thomas A, Cannings R, Gutin A, Pruss D, Reid J, Tavtigian S, Mitchell J, Eldredge G, Scholl T, Miller RM, Bhatnagar S, Adey N, Rubano T, Tusneem N, Robinson R, Feldhaus J, Macalma T, Oliphant A, Briggs S (2002) A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296:92–100" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57061e445">2002</a>; Yu et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2002" title="Yu J, Hu S, Wang J, Wong GK, Li S, Liu B, Deng Y, Dai L, Zhou Y, Zhang X, Cao M, Liu J, Sun J, Tang J, Chen Y, Huang X, Lin W, Ye C, Tong W, Cong L, Geng J, Han Y, Li L, Li W, Hu G, Huang X, Li W, Li J, Liu Z, Li L, Liu J, Qi Q, Liu J, Li L, Li T, Wang X, Lu H, Wu T, Zhu M, Ni P, Han H, Dong W, Ren X, Feng X, Cui P, Li X, Wang H, Xu X, Zhai W, Xu Z, Zhang J, He S, Zhang J, Xu J, Zhang K, Zheng X, Dong J, Zeng W, Tao L, Ye J, Tan J, Ren X, Chen X, He J, Liu D, Tian W, Tian C, Xia H, Bao Q, Li G, Gao H, Cao T, Wang J, Zhao W, Li P, Chen W, Wang X, Zhang Y, Hu J, Wang J, Liu S, Yang J, Zhang G, Xiong Y, Li Z, Mao L, Zhou C, Zhu Z, Chen R, Hao B, Zheng W, Chen S, Guo W, Li G, Liu S, Tao M, Wang J, Zhu L, Yuan L, Yang H (2002) A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296:79–92" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d57061e448">2002</a>). Since then, more and more plant species have been sequenced, including grapevine (Jaillon et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2007" title="Jaillon O, Aury JM, Noel B, Policriti A, Clepet C, Casagrande A, Choisne N, Aubourg S, Vitulo N, Jubin C, Vezzi A, Legeai F, Hugueney P, Dasilva C, Horner D, Mica E, Jublot D, Poulain J, Bruyère C, Billault A, Segurens B, Gouyvenoux M, Ugarte E, Cattonaro F, Anthouard V, Vico V, Del Fabbro C, Alaux M, Di Gaspero G, Dumas V, Felice N, Paillard S, Juman I, Moroldo M, Scalabrin S, Canaguier A, Le Clainche I, Malacrida G, Durand E, Pesole G, Laucou V, Chatelet P, Merdinoglu D, Delledonne M, Pezzotti M, Lecharny A, Scarpelli C, Artiguenave F, Pè ME, Valle G, Morgante M, Caboche M, Adam-Blondon AF, Weissenbach J, Quétier F, Wincker P (2007) The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449:463–467" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57061e451">2007</a>; Velasco et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2007" title="Velasco R, Zharkikh A, Troggio M, Cartwright DA, Cestaro A, Pruss D, Pindo M, Fitzgerald LM, Vezzulli S, Reid J, Malacarne G, Iliev D, Coppola G, Wardell B, Micheletti D, Macalma T, Facci M, Mitchell JT, Perazzolli M, Eldredge G, Gatto P, Oyzerski R, Moretto M, Gutin N, Stefanini M, Chen Y, Segala C, Davenport C, Demattè L, Mraz A, Battilana J, Stormo K, Costa F, Tao Q, Si-Ammour A, Harkins T, Lackey A, Perbost C, Taillon B, Stella A, Solovyev V, Fawcett JA, Sterck L, Vandepoele K, Grando SM, Toppo S, Moser C, Lanchbury J, Bogden R, Skolnick M, Sgaramella V, Bhatnagar SK, Fontana P, Gutin A, Van de Peer Y, Salamini F, Viola R (2007) A high quality draft consensus sequence of the genome of a heterozygous grapevine variety. PLoS One 2:e1326" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d57061e455">2007</a>), papaya (Ming et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Ming R, Hou S, Feng Y, Yu Q, Dionne-Laporte A, Saw JH, Senin P, Wang W, Ly BV, Lewis KL, Salzberg SL, Feng L, Jones MR, Skelton RL, Murray JE, Chen C, Qian W, Shen J, Du P, Eustice M, Tong E, Tang H, Lyons E, Paull RE, Michael TP, Wall K, Rice DW, Albert H, Wang ML, Zhu YJ, Schatz M, Nagarajan N, Acob RA, Guan P, Blas A, Wai CM, Ackerman CM, Ren Y, Liu C, Wang J, Wang J, Na JK, Shakirov EV, Haas B, Thimmapuram J, Nelson D, Wang X, Bowers JE, Gschwend AR, Delcher AL, Singh R, Suzuki JY, Tripathi S, Neupane K, Wei H, Irikura B, Paidi M, Jiang N, Zhang W, Presting G, Windsor A, Navajas-Pérez R, Torres MJ, Feltus FA, Porter B, Li Y, Burroughs AM, Luo MC, Liu L, Christopher DA, Mount SM, Moore PH, Sugimura T, Jiang J, Schuler MA, Friedman V, Mitchell-Olds T, Shippen DE, dePamphilis CW, Palmer JD, Freeling M, Paterson AH, Gonsalves D, Wang L, Alam M (2008) The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452:991–996" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d57061e458">2008</a>), sweet orange (Xu et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Xu Q, Chen LL, Ruan X, Chen D, Zhu A, Chen C, Bertrand D, Jiao WB, Hao BH, Lyon MP, Chen J, Gao S, Xing F, Lan H, Chang JW, Ge X, Lei Y, Hu Q, Miao Y, Wang L, Xiao S, Biswas MK, Zeng W, Guo F, Cao H, Yang X, Xu XW, Cheng YJ, Xu J, Liu JH, Luo OJ, Tang Z, Guo WW, Kuang H, Zhang HY, Roose ML, Nagarajan N, Deng XX, Ruan Y (2013) The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat Genet 47:1435–1442" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d57061e508">2015</a>), and quinoa (Zou et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Zou C, Chen A, Xiao L, Muller HM, Ache P, Haberer G, Zhang M, Jia W, Deng P, Huang R, Lang D, Li F, Zhan D, Wu X, Zhang H, Bohm J, Liu R, Shabala S, Hedrich R, Zhu JK, Zhang H (2017) A high-quality genome assembly of quinoa provides insights into the molecular basis of salt bladder-based salinity tolerance and the exceptional nutritional value. Cell Res 27:1327–1340" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d57061e512">2017</a>). The Apiaceae family contains several vegetable and spice crop species. Recently, the first sequenced genome of carrot was reported (Iorizzo et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Iorizzo M, Ellison S, Senalik D, Zeng P, Satapoomin P, Huang J, Bowman M, Iovene M, Sanseverino W, Cavagnaro P, Yildiz M, Macko-Podgórni A, Moranska E, Grzebelus E, Grzebelus D, Ashrafi H, Zheng Z, Cheng S, Spooner D, Van Deynze A, Simon P (2016) A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat Genet 48:657–666" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d57061e515">2016</a>). The research mainly focused on carotenoid accumulation in carrot. Molecular resources about other physiological processes in carrot are not extensively discussed, and the carrot variety used for sequencing in the work comes from Europe.</p><p>In this study, we report a draft sequence of ‘Kurodagosun’, a variety bred in Japan. ‘Kurodagosun’ has been a major carrot variety in China and Japan. It has a fleshy root with orange color, and is demonstrated to have great applicability. Numerous studies about carrot growth, development, breeding, and secondary metabolism were performed using ‘Kurodagosun’ as materials (Ma et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Ma J, Xu Z, Tan G, Wang F, Xiong A (2017) Distinct transcription profile of genes involved in carotenoid biosynthesis among six different color carrot (Daucus carota L.) cultivars. Acta Biochim Biophys Sin 49:817–826" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d57061e521">2017</a>; Wang et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Wang GL, Que F, Xu ZS, Wang F, Xiong AS (2017) Exogenous gibberellin enhances secondary xylem development and lignification in carrot taproot. Protoplasma 254:839–848" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d57061e524">2017</a>; Liu et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Liu YJ, Wang GL, Ma J, Xu ZS, Wang F, Xiong AS (2018) Transcript profiling of sucrose synthase genes involved in sucrose metabolism among four carrot (Daucus carota L.) cultivars reveals distinct patterns. BMC Plant Biol 18:8" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d57061e527">2018</a>). Here, both the Roche 454 and HiSeq 2000 were used to determine the genome sequence of the carrot ‘DC-27’, a high forced selfing line of ‘Kurodagosun’. The 32 × coverage sequence of ‘DC-27’ genome provided information on the overall organization, gene content and structural components of the DNA. Morphological phenotypes, genome sequence, and other related data from this study are available at <a href="http://apiaceae.njau.edu.cn/carrotdb">http://apiaceae.njau.edu.cn/carrotdb</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plant material and DNA sequencing</h3><p>Carrot material used in this study was the inbred line DC-27, which was derived by forced selfing from ‘Kurodagosun’. Genomic DNA was extracted from the leaves and storage root tissues of ‘DC-27’ according to the instructions of the CTAB DNA extraction method (Rogers and Bendich <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1985" title="Rogers SO, Bendich AJ (1985) Extraction of DNA from milligram amounts of fresh, herbarium and mummified plant tissues. Plant Mol Biol 5:69–76" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d57061e549">1985</a>).</p><p>The genome was sequenced using a combination of Hiseq2000 and Roche 454 GS-FLX<sup>+</sup> platforms. Two insertion sizes of libraries were prepared (fragment lengths of 170 and 500 bp) from ‘DC-27’ DNA and then sequenced through the Solexa-Pipeline (using HiSeq-2000) according to the protocols at the Beijing Genomics Institute-Shenzhen (BGI-Shenzhen). The 454 data set was obtained by sequencing a shotgun library from ‘DC-27’ using the GS-FLX<sup>+</sup> platform. In a typical procedure, a 1 kb shotgun genomic library was constructed using the Rapid Library Preparation kit and protocols at the BGI-Shenzhen and 454 Life Sciences. The library was quantified by fluorometry using Quant-iT Ribo Green reagent (Invitrogen, Carlsbad, CA, USA) prior to emulsion PCR amplification.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec4">Genome assembly</h3><p>The raw reads generated from the Solexa-Pipeline included artificial reads caused by base-calling duplicates and adapter contamination. To facilitate assembly, we performed a series of filtering measures. First, reads with adapter contamination were removed using CutAdapt. Subsequently, the following types of reads were filtered out: (1) reads with more than three unidentified nucleotides, (2) reads with average Phred quality below Q30, and (3) reads with unidentified nucleotides in the first 50 nucleotides. The filtered Hiseq2000 data were assembled with short oligonucleotide analysis package (SOAP) de novo (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html</a>), whereas Roche 454 data were assembled using Newbler Assembler, version 2.8 (Roche). Assemblies were merged using Minimus2 (<a href="http://sourceforge.net/apps/mediawiki/amos/index.php?title=Minimus2">http://sourceforge.net/apps/mediawiki/amos/index.php?title=Minimus2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec5">Assembled scaffolds</h3><p>SSPACE version 1.2 was used to merge contigs into scaffolds (Boetzer et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Boetzer M, Henkel CV, Jansen HJ, Butler D, Pirovano W (2011) Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27:578–579" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57061e589">2011</a>). Subsequent scaffolding of the Minimus2 output was performed by SSPACE using the Hiseq2000 paired-end sequence information.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec6">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Augustus (Stanke et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Stanke M, Steinkamp R, Waack S, Morgenstern B (2004) AUGUSTUS: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Res 32:W309-312" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d57061e600">2004</a>) was used to predict genes. For similarity-based gene prediction, we aligned the protein sequences of three sequenced plants (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i>) onto the carrot genome using TBLASTN with an <i>E</i> value cut off of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>, and then similar genome sequences were aligned against the matching proteins using Genewise for accurately spliced alignments. For EST-based predictions, the EST sequences of three carrot species were aligned against the carrot genome using BLAST (identity ≥ 0.95; coverage ≥ 0.90) to generate spliced alignments (Iorizzo et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Iorizzo M, Senalik DA, Grzebelus D, Bowman M, Cavagnaro PF, Matvienko M, Ashrafi H, Van Deynze A, Simon PW (2011) De novo assembly and characterization of the carrot transcriptome reveals novel genes, new markers, and genetic diversity. BMC Genom 12:389" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d57061e615">2011</a>). Sequences were compared with the Gene Ontology (GO) and Eukaryotic Clusters of Orthologous Groups (KOG) databases to investigate putative functions (Ashburner et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2000" title="Ashburner M, Ball CA, Blake JA, Botstein D, Butler H, Cherry JM, Davis AP, Dolinski K, Dwight SS, Eppig JT, Harris MA, Hill DP, Issel-Tarver L, Kasarskis A, Lewis S, Matese JC, Richardson JE, Ringwald M, Rubin GM, Sherlock G (2000) Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat Genet 25:25–29" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57061e618">2000</a>; Tatusov et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2003" title="Tatusov RL, Fedorova ND, Jackson JD, Jacobs AR, Kiryutin B, Koonin EV, Krylov DM, Mazumder R, Mekhedov SL, Nikolskaya AN, Rao BS, Smirnov S, Sverdlov AV, Vasudevan S, Wolf YI, Yin JJ, Natale DA (2003) The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes. BMC Bioinform 4:41" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d57061e621">2003</a>). Transposable elements in the ‘DC-27’ genome were identified with RepeatMasker (version 3.3.0) and RepeatModler (version 1.0.2). Four types of noncoding RNAs, tRNA, snoRNA, snRNA, and miRNA, were identified by searching against various RNA libraries. Transcription factors in carrot genome were identified and classified into families using the Pfam database (Finn et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2014" title="Finn RD, Bateman A, Clements J, Coggill P, Eberhardt RY, Eddy SR, Heger A, Hetherington K, Holm L, Mistry J, Sonnhammer EL, Tate J, Punta M (2014) Pfam: the protein families database. Nucleic Acids Res 42:D222-D230" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d57061e624">2014</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec7">Identification of potential species-specific genes</h3><p>The OrthoMCL method was adopted to construct gene families for the genes in the three plant species. Proteins with no homologs in the other 21 plant genomes were used for TBLASTN searches against the Plant Unique Transcripts Assembly database (PUTs, <a href="http://www.plantgdb.org/">http://www.plantgdb.org/</a>) to exclude false-positive genes caused by incomplete genome annotation. The potential species-specific genes were rescreened by BLAST searches against the nonredundant protein database in GenBank.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec8">Gene family analyses</h3><p>OrthoMCL package (version 2.0.8) was used to define gene families between carrot and considered species. The protein-coding genes for three species (<i>A. thaliana</i>, TAIR; <i>O. sativa</i>, MSU; <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, JGI) were downloaded from Ensembl (<a href="http://www.ensembl.org">http://www.ensembl.org</a>), and the longest translation was chosen to represent each gene. BlastP was used on all protein sequences with an <i>E</i> value cutoff of 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>. OrthoMCL was then used to classify the BLASTP results into gene families/clusters at inflation value (I) of 1.5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec9">Data access</h3><p>The whole-genome sequences of the ‘DC-27’ project were deposited at CarrotDB under accession version 1.0. Data are also available at our professional website (<a href="http://apiaceae.njau.edu.cn/carrotdb">http://apiaceae.njau.edu.cn/carrotdb</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Sequencing and assembly of ‘DC-27’</h3><p>We generated raw sequences from two next-generation sequencing (NGS) platforms, namely, HiSeq 2000 and Roche 454. A total of 4.7 million shotgun reads were obtained from Roche 454, and 120.0 million Solexa-Pipeline reads were paired, corresponding to 14.04 Gb of raw data. Solexa-Pipeline data were assembled with SOAP de novo, whereas Roche 454 data were assembled using Newbler Assembler. The assemblies were then merged using Minimus2. The genome sequence of ‘DC-27’ was assembled using SOAP de novo, resulting in a final assembly of 371.6 Mb, of which 78.56% were non-gapped sequences. Flow cytometry estimates suggested a genome size of 473 Mb (Arumuganathan and Earle <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1991" title="Arumuganathan K, Earle E (1991) Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol Biol Rep 9:208–218" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d57061e701">1991</a>), consistent with the 17-nucleotide depth distribution (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Fig1">1</a>). Our assembly of 371.6 Mb was ~ 101 Mb less than the estimated genome size of carrot. A total of 32× coverage of paired short read sequences were produced and assembled by HiSeq2000 and Roche 454 technologies with stringent assembly parameters. We assembled the genome of the ‘DC-27’ and analyzed the assembly. The final assembly statistics are summarized in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Tab1">1</a>.</p>
                  <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig1_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig1_HTML.gif" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Depth–frequency distribution</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                
                  <div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly statistics for the ‘DC-27’ genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Gene annotation and gene content of ‘DC-27’</h3><p>We used a combination of de novo gene prediction programs and homology-based methods to predict gene models in the ‘DC-27’ genome. A total of 31,891 genes were predicted. The gene number of ‘DC-27’ was markedly lager than that of <i>Arabidopsis</i> (27,416), but was smaller than that of rice (57,939) and poplar (41,377). The gene models had an average exon length of 235 bp and a mean of 3.53 exons per gene (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Tab2">2</a>). Conserved putative genes of ‘DC-27’ were searched by the BLAST program with <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice and poplar as references. We identified each of the orthologous blocks in the ‘DC-27’ genome using colinearity between orthologs on the genomes of <i>D. carota</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> and found a significant disparity in gene variation. The <i>D. carota</i> genome of ‘DC-27’ retained only 13% of the genes found in <i>A. thaliana</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Fig2">2</a>).</p>
                  <div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Statistics of sequenced plant genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                
                  <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig2_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig2_HTML.gif" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Density of orthologous genes in ‘DC-27’ compared with <i>A. thaliana</i>. The <i>x</i>-axis denotes the physical position of each <i>A. thaliana</i> gene locus. The <i>y</i>-axis denotes the percentage of retained orthologous genes in ‘DC-27’ around each <i>A. thaliana</i> gene, where 500 genes flanking each side of a certain gene locus were analyzed, giving a total window size of 1001 genes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <p>Sequence similarity search was performed against public databases to investigate putative functions. Based on GO database, carrot genes of ‘DC-27’ were annotated to three gene ontology classes: biological process, cellular components and molecular functions, with 115 functional terms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Fig3">3</a>). Molecular functions made up the majority of the GO annotations (49.3% of the total GO IDs), followed by biological processes (32.4%) and cellular components (12.79%).</p>
                  <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig3_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig3_HTML.gif" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Distribution of carrot genes of ‘DC-27’ among GO function classes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <p>By mapping to the KOG database, carrot genes of ‘DC-27’ were classified into 25 function classes, which were in turn grouped into three large categories: (1) information storage and processing ([J], [A], [K], [L], [B]), (2) cellular processes and signing ([D], [Y], [V], [T], [M], [N], [Z], [W], [U], [O]), and (3) metabolism ([C], [G], [E], [F], [H], [I], [P], [Q]) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Fig4">4</a>). The most frequent function clusters in our sequencing were posttranslational modification (10.97%), signal transduction mechanisms (9.01%), and secondary metabolite biosynthesis (5.65%). Genes that were annotated as general function prediction only [R] or function unknown [S], accounting for 27.05% of the dataset were removed in subsequent analyses.</p>
                  <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig4_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig4_HTML.gif" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Clusters of orthologous group classification of carrot genes of ‘DC-27’</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Transcription factors</h3><p>Within the genome of ‘DC-27’, we identified 2826 putative transcription factors distributed in 57 families by sequence comparison to known transcription factor gene families. This number represented 8.86% of the 31,891 predicted ‘DC-27’ protein-coding loci. The percentage of transcription factors in carrot genome of ‘DC-27’ was lower than that in the soybean genome (12.2%; 5671/46,430), but higher than that in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (7.05%), rice (4.8%), poplar (6%) and apple (7%). A total of 57 transcription factor families were found in the carrot genome of ‘DC-27’, which was similar to that in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (64), rice (62), poplar (63) and soybean (63). In the carrot genome, the most highly represented TF families were MYB (386), ABI3-VP1 (266), AP2-EREBP (255), and bHLH (147).</p><p>AP2/EREBP family transcription factors are involved in regulating diverse plant responses, including plant growth and development, regulation of primary and secondary metabolites, as well as abiotic and biotic stresses.Overall, <i>Arabidopsis</i>, rice, poplar and Chinese cabbage genome has 147, 164, 200 and 289 AP2/EREBP transcription factors, respectively, whereas carrot has 255 members (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Fig5">5</a>). The percentage of AP2/EREBP transcription factor genes in carrot that predicted the protein-coding gene was 0.80% (255/31,891), which was higher than that in <i>Arabidopsis</i> (0.54%), poplar (0.41%), rice (0.40%), grapevine (0.43%), and Chinese cabbage (0.70%).</p>
                  <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig5_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig5_HTML.gif" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Distribution of carrot transcription factor genes in different transcription factor families</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Noncoding RNAs</h3><p>In addition to protein-coding genes, we identified 652 transfer RNAs (tRNAs), 301 small nucleolar (snoRNA) RNA, 114 snRNA and 191 miRNA genes in the carrot genome of ‘DC-27’ based on a series of publicly available RNA detection algorithms including tRNA Scan-SE, INFERNAL and snoScan (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Tab3">3</a>).</p>
                  <div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Noncoding RNA genes in the ‘DC-27’ genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Repetitive sequences</h3><p>De novo repeat identification using RepeatMasker and homology analysis against the RepBase library identified repetitive DNA (excluding low-complexity sequences) in 20.959% of the genome of ‘DC-27’, most of which cannot be associated with known transposable element (TE) families. The fraction of repetitive sequences in the genome differ greatly from those of soybean (59%) (Schmutz et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2010" title="Schmutz J, Cannon SB, Schlueter J, Ma J, Mitros T, Nelson W, Hyten DL, Song Q, Thelen JJ, Cheng J, Xu D, Hellsten U, May GD, Yu Y, Sakurai T, Umezawa T, Bhattacharyya MK, Sandhu D, Valliyodan B, Lindquist E, Peto M, Grant D, Shu S, Goodstein D, Barry K, Futrell-Griggs M, Abernathy B, Du J, Tian Z, Zhu L, Gill N, Joshi T, Libault M, Sethuraman A, Zhang XC, Shinozaki K, Nguyen HT, Wing RA, Cregan P, Specht J, Grimwood J, Rokhsar D, Stacey G, Shoemaker RC, Jackson SA (2010) Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463:178–183" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d57061e1734">2010</a>), grapevine (41%) (Jaillon et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2007" title="Jaillon O, Aury JM, Noel B, Policriti A, Clepet C, Casagrande A, Choisne N, Aubourg S, Vitulo N, Jubin C, Vezzi A, Legeai F, Hugueney P, Dasilva C, Horner D, Mica E, Jublot D, Poulain J, Bruyère C, Billault A, Segurens B, Gouyvenoux M, Ugarte E, Cattonaro F, Anthouard V, Vico V, Del Fabbro C, Alaux M, Di Gaspero G, Dumas V, Felice N, Paillard S, Juman I, Moroldo M, Scalabrin S, Canaguier A, Le Clainche I, Malacrida G, Durand E, Pesole G, Laucou V, Chatelet P, Merdinoglu D, Delledonne M, Pezzotti M, Lecharny A, Scarpelli C, Artiguenave F, Pè ME, Valle G, Morgante M, Caboche M, Adam-Blondon AF, Weissenbach J, Quétier F, Wincker P (2007) The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449:463–467" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57061e1737">2007</a>), maize (85%) (Schnable et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2009" title="Schnable PS, Ware D, Fulton RS, Stein JC, Wei F, Pasternak S, Liang C, Zhang J, Fulton L, Graves TA, Minx P, Reily AD, Courtney L, Kruchowski SS, Tomlinson C, Strong C, Delehaunty K, Fronick C, Courtney B, Rock SM, Belter E, Du F, Kim K, Abbott RM, Cotton M, Levy A, Marchetto P, Ochoa K, Jackson SM, Gillam B, Chen W, Yan L, Higginbotham J, Cardenas M, Waligorski J, Applebaum E, Phelps L, Falcone J, Kanchi K, Thane T, Scimone A, Thane N, Henke J, Wang T, Ruppert J, Shah N, Rotter K, Hodges J, Ingenthron E, Cordes M, Kohlberg S, Sgro J, Delgado B, Mead K, Chinwalla A, Leonard S, Crouse K, Collura K, Kudrna D, Currie J, He R, Angelova A, Rajasekar S, Mueller T, Lomeli R, Scara G, Ko A, Delaney K, Wissotski M, Lopez G, Campos D, Braidotti M, Ashley E, Golser W, Kim H, Lee S, Lin J, Dujmic Z, Kim W, Talag J, Zuccolo A, Fan C, Sebastian A, Kramer M, Spiegel L, Nascimento L, Zutavern T, Miller B, Ambroise C, Muller S, Spooner W, Narechania A, Ren L, Wei S, Kumari S, Faga B, Levy MJ, McMahan L, Van Buren P, Vaughn MW, Ying K, Yeh CT, Emrich SJ, Jia Y, Kalyanaraman A, Hsia AP, Barbazuk WB, Baucom RS, Brutnell TP, Carpita NC, Chaparro C, Chia JM, Deragon JM, Estill JC, Fu Y, Jeddeloh JA, Han Y, Lee H, Li P, Lisch DR, Liu S, Liu Z, Nagel DH, McCann MC, SanMiguel P, Myers AM, Nettleton D, Nguyen J, Penning BW, Ponnala L, Schneider KL, Schwartz DC, Sharma A, Soderlund C, Springer NM, Sun Q, Wang H, Waterman M, Westerman R, Wolfgruber TK, Yang L, Yu Y, Zhang L, Zhou S, Zhu Q, Bennetzen JL, Dawe RK, Jiang J, Jiang N, Presting GG, Wessler SR, Aluru S, Martienssen RA, Clifton SW, McCombie WR, Wing RA, Wilson RK (2009) The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326:1112–1115" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d57061e1740">2009</a>), and sorghum (62%) (Paterson et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2009" title="Paterson AH, Bowers JE, Bruggmann R, Dubchak I, Grimwood J, Gundlach H, Haberer G, Hellsten U, Mitros T, Poliakov A, Schmutz J, Spannagl M, Tang H, Wang X, Wicker T, Bharti AK, Chapman J, Feltus FA, Gowik U, Grigoriev IV, Lyons E, Maher CA, Martis M, Narechania A, Otillar RP, Penning BW, Salamov AA, Wang Y, Zhang L, Carpita NC, Freeling M, Gingle AR, Hash CT, Keller B, Klein P, Kresovich S, McCann MC, Ming R, Peterson DG, Mehboob-ur-Rahman, Ware D, Westhoff P, Mayer KF, Messing J, Rokhsar DS (2009) The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457:551–556" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d57061e1743">2009</a>). Classification of the observed transposable elements into known classes revealed that the majority of repetitive sequences were retrotransposons (7.666%), whereas 1.12% of the transposable elements were DNA transposons (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Tab4">4</a>).</p>
                  <div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Classification of repetitive sequences in the draft sequence of the ‘DC-27’ genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Carotenoid pathway in carrot</h3><p>To identify the carotenoid pathway in carrot, the assembled genes were annotated with corresponding Enzyme Commission numbers against the KEGG database. Most of these carotenoid pathway genes were found in our dataset (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#Fig6">6</a>). Here, 39 genes that mainly encoded 16 different enzymes putatively involved in the carotenoid biosynthesis and catabolism pathway were identified in carrot based on the genome sequence of ‘DC-27’ (Supplementary Information).</p>
                  <div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Fig. 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig6_HTML.gif?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs00438-018-1428-3/MediaObjects/438_2018_1428_Fig6_HTML.gif" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Potential of carotenoid pathway in ‘DC-27’</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec17" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec17-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec17">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec17-content"><p>Next-generation sequencing techniques have been recently used in genome sequencing. Higher plants that have been sequenced mainly belong to Cruciferae (<i>Arabidopsis</i> and Chinese cabbage), Poaceae (rice, maize, millet and wheat), Vitaceae (grapevine), Rosaceae (papaya, strawberry and peach), Rutaceae (sweet orange), Cucurbitaceae (watermelon and cucumber), Solanaceae (potato and tomato), Papilionaceae (soybean) and Malvaceae (cotton). Carrot is the mostly widely cultivated member of Apiaceae. Carrot is a good model plant because it has a relatively small genome. Moreover, carrot is an important global vegetable crop that is the largest source of carotenoids and provitamin A in the human diet, which has been extensively discussed in a previous research about carrot genome sequencing (Iorizzo et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Iorizzo M, Ellison S, Senalik D, Zeng P, Satapoomin P, Huang J, Bowman M, Iovene M, Sanseverino W, Cavagnaro P, Yildiz M, Macko-Podgórni A, Moranska E, Grzebelus E, Grzebelus D, Ashrafi H, Zheng Z, Cheng S, Spooner D, Van Deynze A, Simon P (2016) A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat Genet 48:657–666" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2007">2016</a>).</p><p>To further understand the genetic system and plant-specific genes of Apiaceae, we sequenced the genome of carrot line ‘DC-27’ that can help accelerate the process of molecular breeding of carrot. For carrot genome sequencing of ‘DC-27’, we adopted a combination of Roche 454 and HiSeq 2000 methods. The information and genome sequence resources for the carrot genome of ‘DC-27’ reported in this study can enhance both fundamental and applied research on carrot and Apiaceae family plants. Carrot is a biennial plant commonly classified as a cool-season vegetable. Most carrot plants require vernalization to induce flowering (Alessandro et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Alessandro MS, Galmarini CR, Iorizzo M, Simon PW (2013) Molecular mapping of vernalization requirement and fertility restoration genes in carrot. Theor Appl Genet 126:415–423" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2013">2013</a>). Cultivated carrot is a diploid (2<i>n</i> = 18) with a genome size of about 473 Mb, a relatively small genome and similar to rice (Arumuganathan and Earle <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1991" title="Arumuganathan K, Earle E (1991) Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol Biol Rep 9:208–218" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2019">1991</a>; Goff et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2002" title="Goff SA, Ricke D, Lan TH, Presting G, Wang R, Dunn M, Glazebrook J, Sessions A, Oeller P, Varma H, Hadley D, Hutchison D, Martin C, Katagiri F, Lange BM, Moughamer T, Xia Y, Budworth P, Zhong J, Miguel T, Paszkowski U, Zhang S, Colbert M, Sun WL, Chen L, Cooper B, Park S, Wood TC, Mao L, Quail P, Wing R, Dean R, Yu Y, Zharkikh A, Shen R, Sahasrabudhe S, Thomas A, Cannings R, Gutin A, Pruss D, Reid J, Tavtigian S, Mitchell J, Eldredge G, Scholl T, Miller RM, Bhatnagar S, Adey N, Rubano T, Tusneem N, Robinson R, Feldhaus J, Macalma T, Oliphant A, Briggs S (2002) A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. japonica). Science 296:92–100" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2022">2002</a>; Yu et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2002" title="Yu J, Hu S, Wang J, Wong GK, Li S, Liu B, Deng Y, Dai L, Zhou Y, Zhang X, Cao M, Liu J, Sun J, Tang J, Chen Y, Huang X, Lin W, Ye C, Tong W, Cong L, Geng J, Han Y, Li L, Li W, Hu G, Huang X, Li W, Li J, Liu Z, Li L, Liu J, Qi Q, Liu J, Li L, Li T, Wang X, Lu H, Wu T, Zhu M, Ni P, Han H, Dong W, Ren X, Feng X, Cui P, Li X, Wang H, Xu X, Zhai W, Xu Z, Zhang J, He S, Zhang J, Xu J, Zhang K, Zheng X, Dong J, Zeng W, Tao L, Ye J, Tan J, Ren X, Chen X, He J, Liu D, Tian W, Tian C, Xia H, Bao Q, Li G, Gao H, Cao T, Wang J, Zhao W, Li P, Chen W, Wang X, Zhang Y, Hu J, Wang J, Liu S, Yang J, Zhang G, Xiong Y, Li Z, Mao L, Zhou C, Zhu Z, Chen R, Hao B, Zheng W, Chen S, Guo W, Li G, Liu S, Tao M, Wang J, Zhu L, Yuan L, Yang H (2002) A draft sequence of the rice genome (Oryza sativa L. ssp. indica). Science 296:79–92" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2025">2002</a>; Iorizzo et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Iorizzo M, Ellison S, Senalik D, Zeng P, Satapoomin P, Huang J, Bowman M, Iovene M, Sanseverino W, Cavagnaro P, Yildiz M, Macko-Podgórni A, Moranska E, Grzebelus E, Grzebelus D, Ashrafi H, Zheng Z, Cheng S, Spooner D, Van Deynze A, Simon P (2016) A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat Genet 48:657–666" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2029">2016</a>). The nutritional quality and yield of carrot were improved by selective breeding over the centuries.</p><p>Transcription factors are a group of proteins that can bind to specific DNA sequences and enhance or repress the transcriptional rate of their target genes (Sela and Lukatsky <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Sela I, Lukatsky DB (2011) DNA sequence correlations shape nonspecific transcription factor-DNA binding affinity. Biophys J 101:160–166" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2035">2011</a>; Yanagisawa <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1998" title="Yanagisawa S (1998) Transcription factors in plants: physiological functions and regulation of expression. J Plant Res 111:363–371" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2038">1998</a>). They are vital for many plant cellular processes including growth and development, secondary metabolism, and responses to biotic and abiotic stresses (Bi et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Bi H, Luang S, Li Y, Bazanova N, Morran S, Song Z, Perera MA, Hrmova M, Borisjuk N, Lopato S (2016) Identification and characterization of wheat drought-responsive MYB transcription factors involved in the regulation of cuticle biosynthesis. J Exp Bot 67:5363–5380" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2041">2016</a>; Li et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016a" title="Li B, Li Q, Mao X, Li A, Wang J, Chang X, Hao C, Zhang X, Jing R (2016a) Two novel AP2/EREBP transcription factor genes TaPARG have pleiotropic functions on plant architecture and yield-related traits in common wheat. Front Plant Sci 7:1191" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2044">2016a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference b" title="Li C, Qiu J, Yang G, Huang S, Yin J (2016b) Isolation and characterization of a R2R3-MYB transcription factor gene related to anthocyanin biosynthesis in the spathes of Anthurium andraeanum (Hort.). Plant Cell Rep 35:2151–2165" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2047">b</a>). MYB transcription factors can be classified into different groups based on the number and position of MYB repeats. Like most transcription factors, MYB transcription factors have various functions. Among them, R2R3-MYB transcription factors are involved in regulating the accumulation of secondary metabolites in the phenylpropanoid pathway (Huang et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Huang W, Khaldun ABM, Chen J, Zhang C, Lv H, Yuan L, Wang Y (2016) A R2R3-MYB transcription factor regulates the flavonol biosynthetic pathway in a traditional Chinese medicinal plant, Epimedium sagittatum. Front Plant Sci 7:1089" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2051">2016</a>; Li et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016a" title="Li B, Li Q, Mao X, Li A, Wang J, Chang X, Hao C, Zhang X, Jing R (2016a) Two novel AP2/EREBP transcription factor genes TaPARG have pleiotropic functions on plant architecture and yield-related traits in common wheat. Front Plant Sci 7:1191" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2054">2016a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference b" title="Li C, Qiu J, Yang G, Huang S, Yin J (2016b) Isolation and characterization of a R2R3-MYB transcription factor gene related to anthocyanin biosynthesis in the spathes of Anthurium andraeanum (Hort.). Plant Cell Rep 35:2151–2165" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2057">b</a>). Here, MYB proteins account for the largest proportion of carrot transcription factors, suggesting their important roles during carrot growth.</p><p>Noncoding RNA, an RNA molecule that is not translated into a protein, has long been thought to regulate gene expression via sequence-specific interactions with regulatory regions (Zaratiegui et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2007" title="Zaratiegui M, Irvine DV, Martienssen RA (2007) Noncoding RNAs and gene silencing. Cell 128:763–776" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2063">2007</a>). Noncoding RNA genes include highly abundant and functionally important RNAs and are involved in many cellular processes (Abraham et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Abraham M, Dror R, Nussinov R, Wolfson HJ (2008) Analysis and classification of RNA tertiary structures. RNA 14:2274–2289" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2066">2008</a>). In this study, we identified over 1000 noncoding RNAs that may provide valuable genetic resources for gene regulation in carrot.</p><p>Repetitive sequences account for a large component of the eukaryotic genomes (Mehrotra and Goyal <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2014" title="Mehrotra S, Goyal V (2014) Repetitive sequences in plant nuclear DNA: types, distribution, evolution and function. Genom Proteom Bioinform 12:164–171" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2073">2014</a>). They can be divided into different types due to variation in origin, structure, and genomic distribution (Schmidt and Heslop-Harrison <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1998" title="Schmidt T, Heslop-Harrison JS (1998) Genomes, genes and junk: the large-scale organization of plant chromosomes. Trends Plant Sci 3:195–199" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2076">1998</a>). They play important roles in gene expression, heredity, and evolution. It is proposed that the proportion of repetitive sequences is the main factor determining the genome size of different plant species (Mehrotra and Goyal <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2014" title="Mehrotra S, Goyal V (2014) Repetitive sequences in plant nuclear DNA: types, distribution, evolution and function. Genom Proteom Bioinform 12:164–171" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2079">2014</a>). Approximately, 11% of the <i>Arabidopsis</i> genome is made up of repetitive noncoding sequences, whereas this number has increased to 20.959% in the genome of ‘DC-27’. The repetitive sequences identified in carrot genome of ‘DC-27’ would contribute largely to the research focused on carrot genetics and evolution.</p><p>Carotenoids are a heterogeneous group of isoprenoids that are essential components of all photosynthetic organisms (Meier et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Meier S, Tzfadia O, Vallabhaneni R, Gehring C, Wurtzel ET (2011) A transcriptional analysis of carotenoid, chlorophyll and plastidial isoprenoid biosynthesis genes during development and osmotic stress responses in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Syst Biol 5:77" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2088">2011</a>). Carotenoids, such as α-carotene and β-carotene, can serve as vitamin A precursors for human diet. Carrot is a good source of vitamin A precursors (Arango et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2014" title="Arango J, Jourdan M, Geoffriau E, Beyer P, Welsch R (2014) Carotene hydroxylase activity determines the levels of both α-carotene and total carotenoids in orange carrots. Plant Cell 25:2223–2233" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2091">2014</a>; Ma et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Ma J, Xu Z, Tan G, Wang F, Xiong A (2017) Distinct transcription profile of genes involved in carotenoid biosynthesis among six different color carrot (Daucus carota L.) cultivars. Acta Biochim Biophys Sin 49:817–826" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2094">2017</a>). In this study, 39 genes that encode enzymes putatively involved in the carotenoid biosynthesis and catabolism pathway were identified. Recently, a candidate gene regulating carotenoid accumulation in carrot taproot was identified, which suggested that carotenoid accumulation in carrot is controlled at the regulatory level (Iorizzo et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Iorizzo M, Ellison S, Senalik D, Zeng P, Satapoomin P, Huang J, Bowman M, Iovene M, Sanseverino W, Cavagnaro P, Yildiz M, Macko-Podgórni A, Moranska E, Grzebelus E, Grzebelus D, Ashrafi H, Zheng Z, Cheng S, Spooner D, Van Deynze A, Simon P (2016) A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat Genet 48:657–666" href="/article/10.1007/s00438-018-1428-3#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d57061e2097">2016</a>). These findings all contribute to the genetic and molecular studies focusing on carotenoid accumulation in carrot.</p></div></div></section>
